	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Premier League</h1>
	
	<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
		<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
		<div id="contentSub"></div>
		
		
		
		<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
		<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">This article is about the English association football league. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Premier_League_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Premier League (disambiguation)">Premier League (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">



</p>
<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Association football league in England</div>

<p>The <b>Premier League</b> (often referred to as the <b>English Premier League</b> or the <b>EPL</b> outside <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a>) is the top level of the <a href="/wiki/English_football_league_system" title="English football league system">English football league system</a>. Contested by 20 clubs, it operates on a system of <a href="/wiki/Promotion_and_relegation" title="Promotion and relegation">promotion and relegation</a> with the <a href="/wiki/English_Football_League" title="English Football League">English Football League</a> (EFL).
</p><p>The Premier League is a corporation in which the member clubs act as shareholders. Seasons run from August to May with each team playing 38 matches (playing all 19 other teams both home and away).<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> Most games are played on Saturday and Sunday afternoons. The Premier League has featured 47 <a href="/wiki/Association_football_in_England" class="mw-redirect" title="Association football in England">English</a> and two <a href="/wiki/Association_football_in_Wales" class="mw-redirect" title="Association football in Wales">Welsh</a> clubs since its inception, making it a cross-border league.
</p><p>The competition was formed as the <b>FA Premier League</b> on 20 February 1992 following the decision of clubs in the <a href="/wiki/Football_League_First_Division" title="Football League First Division">Football League First Division</a> to break away from the <a href="/wiki/Football_League" class="mw-redirect" title="Football League">Football League</a>, founded in 1888, and take advantage of a lucrative television rights deal.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> The deal was worth £1&#160;billion a year domestically as of 2013–14, with <a href="/wiki/Sky_UK" title="Sky UK">Sky</a> and <a href="/wiki/BT_Group" title="BT Group">BT Group</a> securing the domestic rights to broadcast 116 and 38 games respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> The league generates €2.2&#160;billion per year in domestic and international television rights.<sup id="cite_ref-international_rights_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-international_rights-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Clubs were apportioned central payment revenues of £2.4&#160;billion in 2016–17, with a further £343 million in <a href="/wiki/Premier_League_parachute_and_solidarity_payments" title="Premier League parachute and solidarity payments">solidarity payments</a> to English Football League (EFL) clubs.<sup id="cite_ref-Premier_League_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Premier_League-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Premier League is the most-watched sports league in the world, broadcast in 212 territories to 643&#160;million homes and a potential TV audience of 4.7&#160;billion people.<sup id="cite_ref-Worldviews_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Worldviews-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> For the <a href="/wiki/2018%E2%80%9319_Premier_League" title="2018–19 Premier League">2018–19 season</a> average Premier League match attendance was at 38,181,<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> second to the <a href="/wiki/Bundesliga" title="Bundesliga">Bundesliga</a>'s 43,500,<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> while aggregated attendance across all matches is the highest of any league at 14,508,981.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> Most stadium occupancies are near capacity.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> The Premier League ranks second in the <a href="/wiki/UEFA_coefficient" title="UEFA coefficient">UEFA coefficients</a> of leagues based on performances in European competitions over the past five seasons as of 2018.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/List_of_Premier_League_clubs" title="List of Premier League clubs">Forty-nine clubs</a> have competed since the inception of the Premier League in 1992. Six of them have won the title since then: <a href="/wiki/Manchester_United_F.C." title="Manchester United F.C.">Manchester United</a> (13), <a href="/wiki/Chelsea_F.C." title="Chelsea F.C.">Chelsea</a> (5), <a href="/wiki/Manchester_City_F.C." title="Manchester City F.C.">Manchester City</a> (4), <a href="/wiki/Arsenal_F.C." title="Arsenal F.C.">Arsenal</a> (3), <a href="/wiki/Blackburn_Rovers_F.C." title="Blackburn Rovers F.C.">Blackburn Rovers</a> (1), and <a href="/wiki/Leicester_City_F.C." title="Leicester City F.C.">Leicester City</a> (1). The record of most points in a Premier League season is 100, set by Manchester City in 2017–18.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Origins">Origins</span></h3>
<p>Despite significant European success in the 1970s and early 1980s, the late 1980s marked a low point for English football. <a href="/wiki/Stadium" title="Stadium">Stadiums</a> were crumbling, supporters endured poor facilities, <a href="/wiki/Football_hooliganism" title="Football hooliganism">hooliganism</a> was rife, and English clubs were banned from European competition for five years following the <a href="/wiki/Heysel_Stadium_disaster" title="Heysel Stadium disaster">Heysel Stadium disaster</a> in 1985.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Football_League_First_Division" title="Football League First Division">Football League First Division</a>, the top level of English football since 1888, was behind leagues such as Italy's <a href="/wiki/Serie_A" title="Serie A">Serie A</a> and Spain's <a href="/wiki/La_Liga" title="La Liga">La Liga</a> in attendances and revenues, and several top English players had moved abroad.<sup id="cite_ref-premsitehistory_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-premsitehistory-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>By the turn of the 1990s the downward trend was starting to reverse. At the <a href="/wiki/1990_FIFA_World_Cup" title="1990 FIFA World Cup">1990 FIFA World Cup</a>, England reached the semi-finals; <a href="/wiki/UEFA" title="UEFA">UEFA</a>, European football's governing body, lifted the five-year ban on English clubs playing in European competitions in 1990, resulting in Manchester United lifting the <a href="/wiki/UEFA_Cup_Winners%27_Cup" title="UEFA Cup Winners&#39; Cup">UEFA Cup Winners' Cup</a> in 1991. The <a href="/wiki/Taylor_Report" title="Taylor Report">Taylor Report</a> on stadium safety standards, which proposed expensive upgrades to create all-seater stadiums in the aftermath of the <a href="/wiki/Hillsborough_disaster" title="Hillsborough disaster">Hillsborough disaster</a>, was published in January 1990.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 1980s, major English clubs had begun to transform into business ventures, applying commercial principles to club administration to maximise revenue. <a href="/wiki/Martin_Edwards" title="Martin Edwards">Martin Edwards</a> of Manchester United, <a href="/wiki/Irving_Scholar" title="Irving Scholar">Irving Scholar</a> of Tottenham Hotspur, and <a href="/wiki/David_Dein" title="David Dein">David Dein</a> of Arsenal were among the leaders in this transformation.<sup id="cite_ref-taylor1_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-taylor1-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> The commercial imperative led to the top clubs seeking to increase their power and revenue; the clubs in Division One threatened to break away from the Football League, and in so doing they managed to increase their voting power and gain more favourable financial arrangement, taking a 50% share of all television and sponsorship income in 1986.<sup id="cite_ref-taylor1_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-taylor1-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> They demanded that television companies should pay more for their coverage of football matches,<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> and revenue from television grew in importance. The Football League received £6.3&#160;million for a two-year agreement in 1986, but by 1988, in a deal agreed with ITV, the price rose to £44&#160;million over four years with the leading clubs taking 75% of the cash.<sup id="cite_ref-taylor_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-taylor-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-leicester_tv_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-leicester_tv-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> According to Scholar who was involved in the negotiations of television deals, each of the First Division clubs received only around £25,000 per year from television rights before 1986, this increased to around £50,000 in the 1986 negotiation, then to £600,000 in 1988.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> The 1988 negotiations were conducted under the threat of ten clubs leaving to form a "super league", but they were eventually persuaded to stay with the top clubs taking the lion's share of the deal.<sup id="cite_ref-taylor_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-taylor-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FLhistory_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FLhistory-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>  The negotiations also convinced the bigger clubs that in order to receive enough votes, they needed to take the whole of First Division with them instead of a smaller "super league".<sup id="cite_ref-king_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-king-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> By the beginning of the 1990s, the big clubs again considered breaking away, especially now that they had to fund the cost of stadium upgrade as proposed by the Taylor Report.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foundation">Foundation</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Foundation_of_the_Premier_League" title="Foundation of the Premier League">Foundation of the Premier League</a></div>
<p>In 1990, the managing director of <a href="/wiki/London_Weekend_Television" title="London Weekend Television">London Weekend Television</a> (LWT), <a href="/wiki/Greg_Dyke" title="Greg Dyke">Greg Dyke</a>, met with the representatives of the "big five" football clubs in England (Manchester United, Liverpool, Tottenham, Everton and Arsenal) over a dinner.<sup id="cite_ref-Conn_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Conn-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> The meeting was to pave the way for a break away from <a href="/wiki/The_Football_League" class="mw-redirect" title="The Football League">The Football League</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> Dyke believed that it would be more lucrative for LWT if only the larger clubs in the country were featured on national television and wanted to establish whether the clubs would be interested in a larger share of television rights money.<sup id="cite_ref-Rodrigues_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rodrigues-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> The five clubs agreed with the suggestion and decided to press ahead with it; however, the league would have no credibility without the backing of <a href="/wiki/The_Football_Association" title="The Football Association">The Football Association</a> and so <a href="/wiki/David_Dein" title="David Dein">David Dein</a> of <a href="/wiki/Arsenal_F.C." title="Arsenal F.C.">Arsenal</a> held talks to see whether the FA were receptive to the idea. The FA did not enjoy an amicable relationship with the Football League at the time and considered it as a way to weaken the Football League's position.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> The FA released a report in June 1991, <i>Blueprint for the Future of Football</i>, that supported the plan for Premier League with FA the ultimate authority that would oversee the breakaway league.<sup id="cite_ref-king_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-king-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>At the close of the 1991 season, a proposal was tabled for the establishment of a new league that would bring more money into the game overall. The Founder Members Agreement, signed on 17 July 1991 by the game's top-flight clubs, established the basic principles for setting up the FA Premier League.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> The newly formed top division would have commercial independence from <a href="/wiki/The_Football_Association" title="The Football Association">The Football Association</a> and the Football League, giving the FA Premier League licence to negotiate its own <a href="/wiki/Broadcasting" title="Broadcasting">broadcast</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sponsor_(commercial)" title="Sponsor (commercial)">sponsorship</a> agreements. The argument given at the time was that the extra income would allow English clubs to compete with teams across Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-premsitehistory_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-premsitehistory-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> Although Dyke played a significant role in the creation of the Premier League, Dyke and ITV would lose out in the bidding for broadcast rights as <a href="/wiki/BSkyB" class="mw-redirect" title="BSkyB">BSkyB</a> won with a bid of £304&#160;million over five years with the <a href="/wiki/BBC" title="BBC">BBC</a> awarded the highlights package broadcast on <i><a href="/wiki/Match_of_the_Day" title="Match of the Day">Match of the Day</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Conn_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Conn-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Rodrigues_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rodrigues-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1992, the First Division clubs resigned from the Football League <i><a href="/wiki/En_masse" class="mw-redirect" title="En masse">en masse</a></i> and on 27 May 1992 the FA Premier League was formed as a <a href="/wiki/Private_limited_company_by_shares" class="mw-redirect" title="Private limited company by shares">limited company</a> working out of an office at the Football Association's then headquarters in <a href="/wiki/Lancaster_Gate" title="Lancaster Gate">Lancaster Gate</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-premsitehistory_14-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-premsitehistory-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> This meant a break-up of the 104-year-old Football League that had operated until then with four divisions; the Premier League would operate with a single division and the Football League with three. There was no change in competition format; the same number of teams competed in the top flight, and promotion and relegation between the Premier League and the new First Division remained the same as the old First and Second Divisions with three teams relegated from the league and three promoted.<sup id="cite_ref-FLhistory_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FLhistory-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The league held its first season in <a href="/wiki/1992%E2%80%9393_FA_Premier_League" title="1992–93 FA Premier League">1992–93</a>. It was composed of 22 clubs for that season. The first Premier League goal was scored by <a href="/wiki/Brian_Deane" title="Brian Deane">Brian Deane</a> of Sheffield United in a 2–1 win against Manchester United.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> The 22 inaugural members of the new Premier League were <a href="/wiki/Arsenal_F.C." title="Arsenal F.C.">Arsenal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aston_Villa_F.C." title="Aston Villa F.C.">Aston Villa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Blackburn_Rovers_F.C." title="Blackburn Rovers F.C.">Blackburn Rovers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chelsea_F.C." title="Chelsea F.C.">Chelsea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Coventry_City_F.C." title="Coventry City F.C.">Coventry City</a>, <a href="/wiki/Crystal_Palace_F.C." title="Crystal Palace F.C.">Crystal Palace</a>, <a href="/wiki/Everton_F.C." title="Everton F.C.">Everton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ipswich_Town_F.C." title="Ipswich Town F.C.">Ipswich Town</a>, <a href="/wiki/Leeds_United_A.F.C." class="mw-redirect" title="Leeds United A.F.C.">Leeds United</a>, <a href="/wiki/Liverpool_F.C." title="Liverpool F.C.">Liverpool</a>, <a href="/wiki/Manchester_City_F.C." title="Manchester City F.C.">Manchester City</a>, <a href="/wiki/Manchester_United_F.C." title="Manchester United F.C.">Manchester United</a>, <a href="/wiki/Middlesbrough_F.C." title="Middlesbrough F.C.">Middlesbrough</a>, <a href="/wiki/Norwich_City_F.C." title="Norwich City F.C.">Norwich City</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nottingham_Forest_F.C." title="Nottingham Forest F.C.">Nottingham Forest</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oldham_Athletic_A.F.C." title="Oldham Athletic A.F.C.">Oldham Athletic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Queens_Park_Rangers_F.C." title="Queens Park Rangers F.C.">Queens Park Rangers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sheffield_United_F.C." title="Sheffield United F.C.">Sheffield United</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sheffield_Wednesday_F.C." title="Sheffield Wednesday F.C.">Sheffield Wednesday</a>, <a href="/wiki/Southampton_F.C." title="Southampton F.C.">Southampton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tottenham_Hotspur_F.C." title="Tottenham Hotspur F.C.">Tottenham Hotspur</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Wimbledon_F.C." title="Wimbledon F.C.">Wimbledon</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Luton_Town_F.C." title="Luton Town F.C.">Luton Town</a>, <a href="/wiki/Notts_County_F.C." title="Notts County F.C.">Notts County</a>, and <a href="/wiki/West_Ham_United_F.C." title="West Ham United F.C.">West Ham United</a> were the three teams relegated from the old first division at the end of the 1991–92 season, and did not take part in the inaugural Premier League season.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h3><span id=".22Top_Four.22_dominance_.282000s.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="&quot;Top_Four&quot;_dominance_(2000s)">"Top Four" dominance (2000s)</span></h3>
<p>One significant feature of the Premier League in the mid-2000s was the dominance of the so-called "Top Four" clubs: <a href="/wiki/Arsenal_F.C." title="Arsenal F.C.">Arsenal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chelsea_F.C." title="Chelsea F.C.">Chelsea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Liverpool_F.C." title="Liverpool F.C.">Liverpool</a> and <a href="/wiki/Manchester_United_F.C." title="Manchester United F.C.">Manchester United</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bestofrest_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bestofrest-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> During this decade, they dominated the top four spots, which came with <a href="/wiki/UEFA_Champions_League" title="UEFA Champions League">UEFA Champions League</a> qualification, taking all top-four places in 5 out of 6 seasons from 2003–04 to 2008–09 inclusive, while every season during the 2000s saw the "Big Four" always qualifying for European competition. Following the <a href="/wiki/2003%E2%80%9304_FA_Premier_League" title="2003–04 FA Premier League">2003–04</a> season, Arsenal acquired the nickname "<a href="/wiki/The_Invincibles_(football)" title="The Invincibles (football)">The Invincibles</a>" as they became the first club to complete a Premier League campaign without losing a single game, the only time it has ever happened in the Premier League.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>During the 2000s, only four sides outside the "Top Four" managed to qualify for the Champions League: <a href="/wiki/Leeds_United_A.F.C." class="mw-redirect" title="Leeds United A.F.C.">Leeds United</a> (<a href="/wiki/1999%E2%80%932000_FA_Premier_League" title="1999–2000 FA Premier League">1999–2000</a>), <a href="/wiki/Newcastle_United_F.C." title="Newcastle United F.C.">Newcastle United</a> (<a href="/wiki/2001%E2%80%9302_FA_Premier_League" title="2001–02 FA Premier League">2001–02</a> and <a href="/wiki/2002%E2%80%9303_FA_Premier_League" title="2002–03 FA Premier League">2002–03</a>), <a href="/wiki/Everton_F.C." title="Everton F.C.">Everton</a> (<a href="/wiki/2004%E2%80%9305_FA_Premier_League" title="2004–05 FA Premier League">2004–05</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Tottenham_Hotspur_F.C." title="Tottenham Hotspur F.C.">Tottenham Hotspur</a> (<a href="/wiki/2009%E2%80%9310_Premier_League" title="2009–10 Premier League">2009–10</a>) – each occupying the final Champions League spot, with the exception of Newcastle in the 2002–03 season, who finished third.
</p><p>In May 2008 <a href="/wiki/Kevin_Keegan" title="Kevin Keegan">Kevin Keegan</a> stated that "Top Four" dominance threatened the division, "This league is in danger of becoming one of the most boring but great leagues in the world."<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> Premier League chief executive <a href="/wiki/Richard_Scudamore" title="Richard Scudamore">Richard Scudamore</a> said in defence: "There are a lot of different tussles that go on in the Premier League depending on whether you're at the top, in the middle or at the bottom that make it interesting."<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Between 2005 and 2012, there was a Premier League representative in seven of the eight <a href="/wiki/UEFA_Champions_League" title="UEFA Champions League">Champions League</a> finals, with only "Top Four" clubs reaching that stage. Liverpool (<a href="/wiki/2005_UEFA_Champions_League_Final" title="2005 UEFA Champions League Final">2005</a>), Manchester United (<a href="/wiki/2008_UEFA_Champions_League_Final" title="2008 UEFA Champions League Final">2008</a>) and Chelsea (<a href="/wiki/2012_UEFA_Champions_League_Final" title="2012 UEFA Champions League Final">2012</a>) won the competition during this period, with Arsenal (<a href="/wiki/2006_UEFA_Champions_League_Final" title="2006 UEFA Champions League Final">2006</a>), Liverpool (<a href="/wiki/2007_UEFA_Champions_League_Final" title="2007 UEFA Champions League Final">2007</a>), Chelsea (<a href="/wiki/2008_UEFA_Champions_League_Final" title="2008 UEFA Champions League Final">2008</a>) and Manchester United (<a href="/wiki/2009_UEFA_Champions_League_Final" title="2009 UEFA Champions League Final">2009</a> and <a href="/wiki/2011_UEFA_Champions_League_Final" title="2011 UEFA Champions League Final">2011</a>) all losing Champions League finals.<sup id="cite_ref-ChampionsFinals_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ChampionsFinals-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> Leeds United were the only non-"Top Four" side to reach the semi-finals of the Champions League, in the <a href="/wiki/2000%E2%80%9301_UEFA_Champions_League" title="2000–01 UEFA Champions League">2000–01 season</a>.
</p><p>Additionally, between the 1999–2000 and 2009–10 seasons, four Premier League sides reached <a href="/wiki/UEFA_Europa_League" title="UEFA Europa League">UEFA Cup or Europa League</a> finals, with only Liverpool managing to win the competition in <a href="/wiki/2001_UEFA_Cup_Final" title="2001 UEFA Cup Final">2001</a>. Arsenal (<a href="/wiki/2000_UEFA_Cup_Final" title="2000 UEFA Cup Final">2000</a>), <a href="/wiki/Middlesbrough_F.C." title="Middlesbrough F.C.">Middlesbrough</a> (<a href="/wiki/2006_UEFA_Cup_Final" title="2006 UEFA Cup Final">2006</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Fulham_F.C." title="Fulham F.C.">Fulham</a> (<a href="/wiki/2010_UEFA_Cup_Final" class="mw-redirect" title="2010 UEFA Cup Final">2010</a>) all lost their finals.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Although the group's dominance was reduced to a degree after this period with the emergence of Manchester City and Tottenham, in terms of all time Premier League points won they remain clear by some margin. As of the end of the 2018–19 season – the 27th season of the Premier League – Liverpool, in fourth place in the all-time points table, were over 250 points ahead of the next team, Tottenham Hotspur. They are also the only teams to maintain a winning average of over 50% throughout their entire Premier League tenures.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Emergence_of_the_.22Big_Six.22_.282010s.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Emergence_of_the_&quot;Big_Six&quot;_(2010s)">Emergence of the "Big Six" (2010s)</span></h3>
<p>The years following 2009 marked a shift in the structure of the "Top Four" with <a href="/wiki/Tottenham_Hotspur_F.C." title="Tottenham Hotspur F.C.">Tottenham Hotspur</a> and <a href="/wiki/Manchester_City_F.C." title="Manchester City F.C.">Manchester City</a> both breaking into the top four places on a regular basis, turning the "Top Four" into the "Big Six".<sup id="cite_ref-Jolly_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jolly-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/2009%E2%80%9310_Premier_League" title="2009–10 Premier League">2009–10 season</a>, Tottenham finished fourth and became the first team to break the top four since <a href="/wiki/Everton_F.C." title="Everton F.C.">Everton</a> five years prior.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> Criticism of the gap between an elite group of "super clubs" and the majority of the Premier League has continued, nevertheless, due to their increasing ability to spend more than the other Premier League clubs.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> Manchester City won the title in the <a href="/wiki/2011%E2%80%9312_Premier_League" title="2011–12 Premier League">2011–12 season</a>, becoming the first club outside the "Big Four" to win since <a href="/wiki/Blackburn_Rovers_F.C." title="Blackburn Rovers F.C.">Blackburn Rovers</a> in the <a href="/wiki/1994%E2%80%9395_FA_Premier_League" title="1994–95 FA Premier League">1994–95 season</a>. That season also saw two of the "Big Four" (Chelsea and Liverpool) finish outside the top four places for the first time since that season.<sup id="cite_ref-Jolly_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jolly-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

</td></tr>

<p>With only four UEFA Champions League qualifying places available in the league, greater competition for qualification now exists, albeit from a narrow base of six clubs. If the teams are level on points and goal difference, play off for UEFA Champions League spots will be played in neutral ground. In the following five seasons after the 2011–12 campaign, Manchester United and Liverpool both found themselves outside of the top four three times while Chelsea finished 10th in the 2015–16 season. Arsenal finished 5th in <a href="/wiki/2016%E2%80%9317_Premier_League" title="2016–17 Premier League">2016–17</a>, ending their record run of 20 consecutive top-four finishes.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/2015%E2%80%9316_Premier_League" title="2015–16 Premier League">2015–16 season</a>, the top four was breached by a non-Big Six side for the first time since Everton in 2005. <a href="/wiki/Leicester_City_F.C." title="Leicester City F.C.">Leicester City</a> were the surprise winners of the league, qualifying for the Champions League as a result.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Off the pitch, the "Big Six" wield significant financial power and influence, with these clubs arguing that they should be entitled to a greater share of revenue due to the greater stature of their clubs globally and the attractive football they aim to play.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> Objectors argue that the egalitarian revenue structure in the Premier League helps to maintain a competitive league which is vital for its future success.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/2016%E2%80%9317_Premier_League" title="2016–17 Premier League">2016–17</a> <a href="/wiki/Deloitte_Football_Money_League#2016–17" title="Deloitte Football Money League">Deloitte Football Money League</a> report showed the financial disparity between the "Big Six" and the rest of the division. All of the "Big Six" had revenues greater than €350&#160;million, with Manchester United having the largest revenue in the league at €676.3&#160;million. Leicester City was the closest club to the "Big Six" in terms of revenue, recording a figure of €271.1&#160;million for that season – helped by participation in the Champions League. The eighth largest revenue generator West Ham, who didn't play in European competition, had revenues of €213.3&#160;million, nearly half of the club with the fifth largest revenue, Liverpool (€424.2&#160;million).<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>  A substantial part of the clubs' revenue by then came from television broadcast deals, with the biggest clubs each taking from around £150&#160;million to nearly £200&#160;million in the 2016–17 season from such deals.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> In Deloitte's 2019 report, all of the "Big Six" were in the top ten of the world's richest clubs.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Development">Development</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Premier_League_seasons" title="List of Premier League seasons">List of Premier League seasons</a></div>
<p>The number of clubs was reduced to 20, down from 22, in <a href="/wiki/1994%E2%80%9395_FA_Premier_League" title="1994–95 FA Premier League">1995</a> when four teams were relegated from the league and only two teams promoted.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-evolved_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-evolved-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> The top flight had only been expanded to 22 teams at the start of the <a href="/wiki/1991%E2%80%9392_Football_League" title="1991–92 Football League">1991–92 season</a> – the year prior to the formation of the Premier League.<sup id="cite_ref-evolved_53-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-evolved-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On 8 June 2006, FIFA requested that all major European leagues, including Italy's <a href="/wiki/Serie_A" title="Serie A">Serie A</a> and Spain's <a href="/wiki/La_Liga" title="La Liga">La Liga</a>, be reduced to 18 teams by the start of the <a href="/wiki/2007%E2%80%9308_Premier_League" title="2007–08 Premier League">2007–08 season</a>. The Premier League responded by announcing their intention to resist such a reduction.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> Ultimately, the 2007–08 season kicked off again with 20 teams.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The league changed its name from the <i>FA Premier League</i> to simply the <i>Premier League</i> in 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Corporate_structure">Corporate structure</span></h2>
<p>The Football Association Premier League Ltd (FAPL)<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> is operated as a corporation and is owned by the 20 member clubs. Each club is a <a href="/wiki/Shareholder" title="Shareholder">shareholder</a>, with one vote each on issues such as rule changes and contracts. The clubs elect a chairman, chief executive, and board of directors to oversee the daily operations of the league.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> The Football Association is not directly involved in the day-to-day operations of the Premier League, but has veto power as a special shareholder during the election of the chairman and chief executive and when new rules are adopted by the league.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The current chairman is <a href="/wiki/Dave_Richards" title="Dave Richards">Sir Dave Richards</a>, who was appointed in April 1999, and the chief executive is <a href="/wiki/Richard_Scudamore" title="Richard Scudamore">Richard Scudamore</a>, appointed in November 1999.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> The former chairman and chief executive, <a href="/wiki/John_Quinton_(banker)" title="John Quinton (banker)">John Quinton</a> and <a href="/wiki/Peter_Leaver" title="Peter Leaver">Peter Leaver</a>, were forced to resign in March 1999 after awarding consultancy contracts to former Sky executives <a href="/wiki/Sam_Chisholm" title="Sam Chisholm">Sam Chisholm</a> and David Chance.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Rick_Parry" title="Rick Parry">Rick Parry</a> was the league's first chief executive.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> On 13 November 2018, <a href="/wiki/Susanna_Dinnage" title="Susanna Dinnage">Susanna Dinnage</a> was announced as Scudamore's successor due to start in early 2019.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Premier League sends representatives to UEFA's <a href="/wiki/European_Club_Association" title="European Club Association">European Club Association</a>, the number of clubs and the clubs themselves chosen according to <a href="/wiki/UEFA_coefficients" class="mw-redirect" title="UEFA coefficients">UEFA coefficients</a>. For the 2012–13 season the Premier League has 10 representatives in the Association: Arsenal, Aston Villa, Chelsea, Everton, Fulham, Liverpool, Manchester City, Manchester United, Newcastle United and Tottenham Hotspur.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> The European Club Association is responsible for electing three members to UEFA's Club Competitions Committee, which is involved in the operations of UEFA competitions such as the <a href="/wiki/UEFA_Champions_League" title="UEFA Champions League">Champions League</a> and <a href="/wiki/UEFA_Europa_League" title="UEFA Europa League">UEFA Europa League</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Competition_format">Competition format</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Competition">Competition</span></h3>
<p>There are 20 clubs in the Premier League. During the course of a season (from August to May) each club plays the others twice (a double <a href="/wiki/Round-robin_tournament" title="Round-robin tournament">round-robin system</a>), once at their home stadium and once at that of their opponents', for 38 games. Teams receive <a href="/wiki/Three_points_for_a_win" title="Three points for a win">three points for a win</a> and one point for a draw. No points are awarded for a loss. Teams are <a href="/wiki/Group_tournament_ranking_system" title="Group tournament ranking system">ranked</a> by total points, then <a href="/wiki/Goal_difference" title="Goal difference">goal difference</a>, and then goals scored. If still equal, teams are deemed to occupy the same position. If there is a tie for the championship, for relegation, or for qualification to other competitions, a play-off match at a neutral venue decides rank.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Promotion_and_relegation">Promotion and relegation</span></h3>
<p>A system of <a href="/wiki/Promotion_and_relegation" title="Promotion and relegation">promotion and relegation</a> exists between the Premier League and the <a href="/wiki/EFL_Championship" title="EFL Championship">EFL Championship</a>. The three lowest placed teams in the Premier League are relegated to the Championship, and the top two teams from the Championship promoted to the Premier League,<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> with an additional team promoted after a series of play-offs involving the third, fourth, fifth and sixth placed clubs.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> The Premier League had 22 teams when it began in 1992, but this was reduced to the present 20-team format in 1995.<sup id="cite_ref-evolved_53-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-evolved-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Clubs">Clubs</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Premier_League_clubs" title="List of Premier League clubs">List of Premier League clubs</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Performance_record_of_clubs_in_the_Premier_League" title="Performance record of clubs in the Premier League">Performance record of clubs in the Premier League</a> and <a href="/wiki/All-time_FA_Premier_League_table" class="mw-redirect" title="All-time FA Premier League table">All-time FA Premier League table</a></div>
<p>49 clubs have played in the Premier League from its inception in 1992, up to and including the <a href="/wiki/2018%E2%80%9319_Premier_League" title="2018–19 Premier League">2018–19</a> season.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Cardiff_City_F.C." title="Cardiff City F.C.">Cardiff City</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fulham_F.C." title="Fulham F.C.">Fulham</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Huddersfield_Town_A.F.C." title="Huddersfield Town A.F.C.">Huddersfield Town</a> were relegated to the <a href="/wiki/2019%E2%80%9320_EFL_Championship" title="2019–20 EFL Championship">EFL Championship</a> for the 2019–20 season, while <a href="/wiki/Norwich_City_F.C." title="Norwich City F.C.">Norwich City</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sheffield_United_F.C." title="Sheffield United F.C.">Sheffield United</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aston_Villa_F.C." title="Aston Villa F.C.">Aston Villa</a>, as winners, runners-up and play-off final winners respectively, were promoted from the <a href="/wiki/2018%E2%80%9319_EFL_Championship" title="2018–19 EFL Championship">2018–19 EFL Championship</a> season.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/A.F.C._Bournemouth" title="A.F.C. Bournemouth">Bournemouth</a> and <a href="/wiki/Brighton_and_Hove_Albion_F.C." class="mw-redirect" title="Brighton and Hove Albion F.C.">Brighton and Hove Albion</a> are the only clubs to have remained in the Premier League since their first promotion, having been in 5 and 3 seasons (out of 28) respectively.</li></ul>
<p><sup>a</sup>: Founding member of the Premier League<br />
<sup>b</sup>: Never been relegated from Premier League<br />
<sup>c</sup>: One of the original 12 Football League teams<br />

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Non-English_clubs">Non-English clubs</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_association_football_clubs_playing_in_the_league_of_another_country" title="List of association football clubs playing in the league of another country">List of association football clubs playing in the league of another country</a></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Wales">Wales</span></h4>
<p>In 2011, a Welsh club participated in the Premier League for the first time after <a href="/wiki/Swansea_City_A.F.C." title="Swansea City A.F.C.">Swansea City</a> gained promotion.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> The first Premier League match to be played outside England was Swansea City's home match at the <a href="/wiki/Liberty_Stadium" title="Liberty Stadium">Liberty Stadium</a> against <a href="/wiki/Wigan_Athletic_F.C." title="Wigan Athletic F.C.">Wigan Athletic</a> on 20 August 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> The number of Welsh clubs in the Premier League increased to two in 2013–14, as <a href="/wiki/Cardiff_City_F.C." title="Cardiff City F.C.">Cardiff City</a> gained promotion,<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> but they were relegated after their maiden season.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> Cardiff were promoted again in <a href="/wiki/2017%E2%80%9318_EFL_Championship" title="2017–18 EFL Championship">2017–18</a> but the number of Welsh clubs remained the same for the <a href="/wiki/2018%E2%80%9319_Premier_League" title="2018–19 Premier League">2018–19 Premier League</a> season as Swansea City were relegated from the Premier League in <a href="/wiki/2017%E2%80%9318_Premier_League" title="2017–18 Premier League">2017–18</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> Following Cardiff City's relegation in <a href="/wiki/2018%E2%80%9319_Premier_League" title="2018–19 Premier League">2018–19</a> there are currently no Welsh clubs participating in the Premier League.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Because they are members of the <a href="/wiki/Football_Association_of_Wales" title="Football Association of Wales">Football Association of Wales</a> (FAW), the question of whether clubs like Swansea should represent England or Wales in European competitions has caused <a href="/wiki/Football_in_England#Welsh_clubs" title="Football in England">long-running discussions</a> in <a href="/wiki/UEFA" title="UEFA">UEFA</a>. Swansea took one of England's three available places in the <a href="/wiki/UEFA_Europa_League" title="UEFA Europa League">Europa League</a> in 2013–14 by winning the <a href="/wiki/Football_League_Cup" class="mw-redirect" title="Football League Cup">League Cup</a> in 2012–13.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> The right of Welsh clubs to take up such English places was in doubt until UEFA clarified the matter in March 2012, allowing them to participate.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Scotland_and_Ireland">Scotland and Ireland</span></h4>
<p>Participation in the Premier League by some Scottish or Irish clubs has sometimes been discussed, but without result.
The idea came closest to reality in 1998, when <a href="/wiki/Wimbledon_F.C." title="Wimbledon F.C.">Wimbledon</a> received Premier League approval to <a href="/wiki/Relocation_of_Wimbledon_F.C._to_Milton_Keynes" title="Relocation of Wimbledon F.C. to Milton Keynes">relocate to Dublin, Ireland</a>, but the move was blocked by the <a href="/wiki/Football_Association_of_Ireland" title="Football Association of Ireland">Football Association of Ireland</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-thewimbledonwehave_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-thewimbledonwehave-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-mihirbose_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mihirbose-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-DublinDonsIndo230198_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DublinDonsIndo230198-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-DublinDonsIndo100698_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DublinDonsIndo100698-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> Additionally, the media occasionally discusses the idea that Scotland's two biggest teams, <a href="/wiki/Celtic_F.C." title="Celtic F.C.">Celtic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rangers_F.C." title="Rangers F.C.">Rangers</a>, should or will take part in the Premier League, but nothing has come of these discussions.<sup id="cite_ref-strachan_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-strachan-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="International_competitions">International competitions</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Qualification_for_European_competitions">Qualification for European competitions</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/UEFA_coefficient#Country_coefficient" title="UEFA coefficient">UEFA country coefficients</a></div>
<h4><span id="Qualification_criteria_for_2020.E2.80.9321"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Qualification_criteria_for_2020–21">Qualification criteria for 2020–21</span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/2020%E2%80%9321_UEFA_Champions_League#Association_team_allocation" title="2020–21 UEFA Champions League">2020–21 UEFA Champions League§Association team allocation</a></div>
<p>The top four teams in the Premier League qualify for the subsequent season's <a href="/wiki/UEFA_Champions_League" title="UEFA Champions League">UEFA Champions League</a> group stage. The winners of the <a href="/wiki/UEFA_Champions_League" title="UEFA Champions League">UEFA Champions League</a> and <a href="/wiki/UEFA_Europa_League" title="UEFA Europa League">UEFA Europa League</a> also qualify for the subsequent season's <a href="/wiki/UEFA_Champions_League" title="UEFA Champions League">UEFA Champions League</a> group stage. If this means six Premier League teams qualify, then the fourth-placed team in the Premier League instead plays in the <a href="/wiki/UEFA_Europa_League" title="UEFA Europa League">UEFA Europa League</a>, as any single nation is limited to a maximum of 5 teams.
</p><p>The fifth-placed team in the Premier League, as well as the winner of the <a href="/wiki/FA_Cup" title="FA Cup">FA Cup</a>, qualifies for the subsequent season's <a href="/wiki/UEFA_Europa_League" title="UEFA Europa League">UEFA Europa League</a> group stage, but if the winner also finished in the top five places in the Premier League, then this place reverts to the team that finished sixth. The winner of the <a href="/wiki/EFL_Cup" title="EFL Cup">EFL Cup</a> qualifies for the subsequent season's <a href="/wiki/UEFA_Europa_League" title="UEFA Europa League">UEFA Europa League</a> second qualifying round, but if the winner already qualified for a <a href="/wiki/UEFA" title="UEFA">UEFA</a> competition via the Premier League or FA Cup, then this place reverts to the team that finished sixth in the Premier League, or seventh if the <a href="/wiki/FA_Cup" title="FA Cup">FA Cup</a> result already caused the sixth-placed team to qualify.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The number of places allocated to English clubs in <a href="/wiki/UEFA" title="UEFA">UEFA</a> competitions is dependent upon the position a country holds in the <a href="/wiki/UEFA_coefficient#Country_coefficient" title="UEFA coefficient">UEFA country coefficients</a>, which are calculated based upon the performance of teams in <a href="/wiki/UEFA" title="UEFA">UEFA</a> competitions in the previous five years. Currently the ranking of England (and <a href="/wiki/De_facto" title="De facto">de facto</a> the Premier League) is 2nd behind <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a>.
</p>

<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Previous_seasons">Previous seasons</span></h4>
<p>An <a href="/wiki/Liverpool_F.C._2005%E2%80%9306_UEFA_Champions_League_qualification" title="Liverpool F.C. 2005–06 UEFA Champions League qualification">exception to the usual European qualification system</a> happened in 2005, after <a href="/wiki/Liverpool_F.C." title="Liverpool F.C.">Liverpool</a> won the Champions League the year before, but did not finish in a Champions League qualification place in the Premier League that season. UEFA gave special dispensation for Liverpool to enter the Champions League, giving England five qualifiers.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> UEFA subsequently ruled that the defending champions qualify for the competition the following year regardless of their domestic league placing. However, for those leagues with four entrants in the Champions League, this meant that if the Champions League winner finished outside the top four in its domestic league, it would qualify at the expense of the fourth-placed team in the league. At that time, no association could have more than four entrants in the Champions League.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> This occurred in 2012, when Chelsea – who had <a href="/wiki/2012_UEFA_Champions_League_Final" title="2012 UEFA Champions League Final">won</a> the Champions League that summer, but finished sixth in the league – qualified for the Champions League in place of Tottenham Hotspur, who went into the Europa League.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Performance_in_international_competition">Performance in international competition</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/English_football_clubs_in_international_competitions" title="English football clubs in international competitions">English football clubs in international competitions</a></div>
<p>Between the 1992–93 and the 2018–19 seasons, Premier League clubs won the <a href="/wiki/UEFA_Champions_League" title="UEFA Champions League">UEFA Champions League</a> five times (and had seven runners-up), behind Spain's <a href="/wiki/La_Liga" title="La Liga">La Liga</a> with eleven wins, level with Italy's <a href="/wiki/Serie_A" title="Serie A">Serie A</a>, and ahead of, among others, Germany's <a href="/wiki/Bundesliga" title="Bundesliga">Bundesliga</a> with three wins.<sup id="cite_ref-ChampionsFinals_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ChampionsFinals-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/FIFA_Club_World_Cup" title="FIFA Club World Cup">FIFA Club World Cup</a> (originally called the FIFA Club World Championship) <a href="/wiki/List_of_FIFA_Club_World_Cup_finals#Results_by_nation" title="List of FIFA Club World Cup finals">has been won</a> once by a Premier League club (Manchester United in <a href="/wiki/2008_FIFA_Club_World_Cup" title="2008 FIFA Club World Cup">2008</a>),<sup id="cite_ref-FIFA_2008_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FIFA_2008-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> with two runners-up (Liverpool in <a href="/wiki/2005_FIFA_Club_World_Championship" title="2005 FIFA Club World Championship">2005</a>, Chelsea in <a href="/wiki/2012_FIFA_Club_World_Cup" title="2012 FIFA Club World Cup">2012</a>),<sup id="cite_ref-FIFA_2005_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FIFA_2005-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FIFA_2012_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FIFA_2012-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> behind Spain's La Liga with seven wins,<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> Brazil's <a href="/wiki/Campeonato_Brasileiro_S%C3%A9rie_A" title="Campeonato Brasileiro Série A">Brasileirão</a> with four wins,<sup id="cite_ref-FIFA_2005_92-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FIFA_2005-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FIFA_2012_93-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FIFA_2012-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FIFA_2000_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FIFA_2000-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FIFA_2006_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FIFA_2006-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> and Italy's Serie A with two wins.<sup id="cite_ref-FIFA_2007_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FIFA_2007-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FIFA_2010_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FIFA_2010-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sponsorship">Sponsorship</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/English_football_sponsorship" title="English football sponsorship">English football sponsorship</a></div>
<p>From 1993 to 2016, the Premier League had <a href="/wiki/Title_sponsor" class="mw-redirect" title="Title sponsor">title sponsorship</a> rights sold to two companies, which were <a href="/wiki/Carling_brewery" class="mw-redirect" title="Carling brewery">Carling brewery</a> and <a href="/wiki/Barclays_Bank_PLC" class="mw-redirect" title="Barclays Bank PLC">Barclays Bank PLC</a>; <a href="/wiki/Barclays" title="Barclays">Barclays</a> was the most recent title sponsor, having sponsored the Premier League from 2001 until 2016 (until 2004, the title sponsorship was held through its <a href="/wiki/Barclaycard" title="Barclaycard">Barclaycard</a> brand before shifting to its main banking brand in 2004).<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<td>2001–2004</td>
<td><a href="/wiki/Barclaycard" title="Barclaycard">Barclaycard</a></td>
<td>FA Barclaycard Premiership<sup id="cite_ref-premsitehistory_14-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-premsitehistory-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>2004–2007</td>
<td rowspan="2"><a href="/wiki/Barclays" title="Barclays">Barclays</a></td>
<td>FA Barclays Premiership
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>2007–2016</td>
<td>Barclays Premier League<sup id="cite_ref-premsitehistory_14-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-premsitehistory-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>2016–</td>
<td>No sponsor</td>
<td>Premier League
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Barclays' deal with the Premier League expired at the end of the 2015–16 season. The FA announced on 4 June 2015 that it would not pursue any further title sponsorship deals for the Premier League, arguing that they wanted to build a "clean" brand for the competition more in line with those of <a href="/wiki/Major_professional_sports_leagues_in_the_United_States_and_Canada" title="Major professional sports leagues in the United States and Canada">major U.S. sports leagues</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-espn-notitlesponsor_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-espn-notitlesponsor-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As well as sponsorship for the league itself, the Premier League has a number of official partners and suppliers.<sup id="cite_ref-partners_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-partners-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> The official ball supplier for the league is <a href="/wiki/Nike,_Inc." title="Nike, Inc.">Nike</a> who have had the contract since the 2000–01 season when they took over from <a href="/wiki/Mitre_Sports_International" title="Mitre Sports International">Mitre</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> Under its <i>Merlin</i> brand, <a href="/wiki/Topps#Topps_Europe_Ltd_Products" title="Topps">Topps</a> held the licence to produce collectables for the Premier League between 1994 and 2019 including stickers (for their <a href="/wiki/Sticker_album" title="Sticker album">sticker album</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Trading_card#Association_football" title="Trading card">trading cards</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Topps_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Topps-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> Launched in the 2007–08 season, Topps’ Match Attax, the official Premier League trading card game, is the best selling boys collectable in the UK, and is also the biggest selling sports trading card game in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-Topps_104-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Topps-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> In October 2018, <a href="/wiki/Panini_Group" title="Panini Group">Panini</a> were awarded the licence to produce collectables from the 2019–20 season.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup>
Since 2017, the chocolate company <a href="/wiki/Cadbury" title="Cadbury">Cadbury</a> is the official snack partner of the Premier League, and sponsors the <a href="/wiki/Premier_League_Golden_Boot" title="Premier League Golden Boot">Premier League Golden Boot</a> and <a href="/wiki/Premier_League_Golden_Glove" title="Premier League Golden Glove">Premier League Golden Glove</a> awards.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Finances">Finances</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_English_football_club_owners#Premier_League" class="mw-redirect" title="List of English football club owners">List of Premier League football club owners</a></div>
<p>The Premier League has the highest revenue of any football league in the world, with total club revenues of €2.48&#160;billion in 2009–10.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Revenues_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Revenues-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> In 2013–14, due to improved television revenues and cost controls, the Premier League had net profits in excess of £78&#160;million, exceeding all other football leagues.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> In 2010 the Premier League was awarded the <a href="/wiki/Queen%27s_Awards_for_Enterprise" title="Queen&#39;s Awards for Enterprise">Queen's Award for Enterprise</a> in the International Trade category for its outstanding contribution to international trade and the value it brings to English football and the United Kingdom's broadcasting industry.<sup id="cite_ref-Enterprise_Award_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Enterprise_Award-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Premier League includes some of the richest football clubs in the world. Deloitte's "<a href="/wiki/Deloitte_Football_Money_League" title="Deloitte Football Money League">Football Money League</a>" listed seven Premier League clubs in the top 20 for the 2009–10 season,<sup id="cite_ref-Deloitte0809_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Deloitte0809-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> and all 20 clubs were in the top 40 globally by the end of the 2013–14 season, largely as a result of increased broadcasting revenue.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> From 2013, the league generates €2.2&#160;billion per year in domestic and international television rights.<sup id="cite_ref-international_rights_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-international_rights-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Premier League clubs agreed in principle in December 2012, to radical new cost controls. The two proposals consist of a break-even rule and a cap on the amount clubs can increase their wage bill by each season. With the new television deals on the horizon, momentum has been growing to find ways of preventing the majority of the cash going straight to players and agents.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Central payments for the 2016–17 season amounted to £2,398,515,773 across the 20 clubs, with each team receiving a flat participation fee of £35,301,989 and additional payments for TV broadcasts (£1,016,690 for general UK rights to match highlights, £1,136,083 for each live UK broadcast of their games and £39,090,596 for all overseas rights), commercial rights (a flat fee of £4,759,404) and a notional measure of "merit" which was based upon final league position.<sup id="cite_ref-Premier_League_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Premier_League-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> The merit component was a nominal sum of £1,941,609 multiplied by each finishing place, counted from the foot of the table (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Burnley_F.C." title="Burnley F.C.">Burnley</a> finished 16th in May 2017, five places counting upwards, and received 5 × £1,941,609 = £9,708,045 merit payment).<sup id="cite_ref-Premier_League_5-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Premier_League-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Media_coverage">Media coverage</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/English_football_on_television" title="English football on television">English football on television</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_Premier_League_broadcasters" title="List of Premier League broadcasters">List of Premier League broadcasters</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Broadcasting_and_the_foundation_of_the_Premier_League" title="Broadcasting and the foundation of the Premier League">Broadcasting and the foundation of the Premier League</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="United_Kingdom_and_Ireland">United Kingdom and Ireland</span></h3>

<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hazard_taking_on_Howson.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Hazard_taking_on_Howson.jpg/220px-Hazard_taking_on_Howson.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="124" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Hazard_taking_on_Howson.jpg/330px-Hazard_taking_on_Howson.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Hazard_taking_on_Howson.jpg/440px-Hazard_taking_on_Howson.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4608" data-file-height="2592" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hazard_taking_on_Howson.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Eden_Hazard" title="Eden Hazard">Eden Hazard</a> in possession of the ball during a 2012 match between <a href="/wiki/Chelsea_F.C." title="Chelsea F.C.">Chelsea</a> and <a href="/wiki/Norwich_City_F.C." title="Norwich City F.C.">Norwich City</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Television has played a major role in the history of the Premier League. The League's decision to assign broadcasting rights to <a href="/wiki/Sky_plc" class="mw-redirect" title="Sky plc">BSkyB</a> in 1992 was at the time a radical decision, but one that has paid off. At the time pay television was an almost untested proposition in the UK market, as was charging fans to watch live televised football. However, a combination of Sky's strategy, the quality of Premier League football and the public's appetite for the game has seen the value of the Premier League's TV rights soar.<sup id="cite_ref-leicester_tv_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-leicester_tv-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Premier League sells its television rights on a collective basis. This is in contrast to some other European Leagues, including La Liga, in which each club sells its rights individually, leading to a much higher share of the total income going to the top few clubs.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> The money is divided into three parts:<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> half is divided equally between the clubs; one quarter is awarded on a merit basis based on final league position, the top club getting twenty times as much as the bottom club, and equal steps all the way down the table; the final quarter is paid out as facilities fees for games that are shown on television, with the top clubs generally receiving the largest shares of this. The income from overseas rights is divided equally between the twenty clubs.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The first Sky television rights agreement was worth £304&#160;million over five seasons.<sup id="cite_ref-fgrc_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fgrc-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> The next contract, negotiated to start from the 1997–98 season, rose to £670&#160;million over four seasons.<sup id="cite_ref-fgrc_118-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fgrc-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> The third contract was a £1.024&#160;billion deal with BSkyB for the three seasons from 2001 to 2002 to 2003–04. The league brought in £320&#160;million from the sale of its international rights for the three-year period from 2004 to 2005 to 2006–07. It sold the rights itself on a territory-by-territory basis.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> Sky's monopoly was broken from August 2006 when <a href="/wiki/Setanta_Sports" title="Setanta Sports">Setanta Sports</a> was awarded rights to show two out of the six packages of matches available. This occurred following an insistence by the <a href="/wiki/European_Commission" title="European Commission">European Commission</a> that exclusive rights should not be sold to one television company. Sky and Setanta paid £1.7&#160;billion, a two-thirds increase which took many commentators by surprise as it had been widely assumed that the value of the rights had levelled off following many years of rapid growth. Setanta also hold rights to a live 3&#160;pm match solely for Irish viewers. The <a href="/wiki/BBC" title="BBC">BBC</a> has retained the rights to show highlights for the same three seasons (on <i><a href="/wiki/Match_of_the_Day" title="Match of the Day">Match of the Day</a></i>) for £171.6&#160;million, a 63&#160;per&#160;cent increase on the £105&#160;million it paid for the previous three-year period.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> Sky and <a href="/wiki/BT_Group" title="BT Group">BT</a> have agreed to jointly pay £84.3&#160;million for delayed television rights to 242 games (that is the right to broadcast them in full on television and over the internet) in most cases for a period of 50 hours after 10&#160;pm on matchday.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> Overseas television rights fetched £625&#160;million, nearly double the previous contract.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> The total raised from these deals is more than £2.7&#160;billion, giving Premier League clubs an average media income from league games of around £40&#160;million-a-year from 2007 to 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:2009-3-14_ManUtd_vs_LFC_Ronaldo_Freekick_Back.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/2009-3-14_ManUtd_vs_LFC_Ronaldo_Freekick_Back.JPG/220px-2009-3-14_ManUtd_vs_LFC_Ronaldo_Freekick_Back.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/2009-3-14_ManUtd_vs_LFC_Ronaldo_Freekick_Back.JPG/330px-2009-3-14_ManUtd_vs_LFC_Ronaldo_Freekick_Back.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/2009-3-14_ManUtd_vs_LFC_Ronaldo_Freekick_Back.JPG/440px-2009-3-14_ManUtd_vs_LFC_Ronaldo_Freekick_Back.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3008" data-file-height="2000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:2009-3-14_ManUtd_vs_LFC_Ronaldo_Freekick_Back.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Cristiano_Ronaldo" title="Cristiano Ronaldo">Cristiano Ronaldo</a> preparing to take a free kick in a 2009 match between <a href="/wiki/Manchester_United_F.C." title="Manchester United F.C.">Manchester United</a> and <a href="/wiki/Liverpool_F.C." title="Liverpool F.C.">Liverpool</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The TV rights agreement between the Premier League and Sky has faced accusations of being a cartel, and a number of court cases have arisen as a result.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> An investigation by the <a href="/wiki/Office_of_Fair_Trading" title="Office of Fair Trading">Office of Fair Trading</a> in 2002 found BSkyB to be dominant within the pay TV sports market, but concluded that there were insufficient grounds for the claim that BSkyB had abused its dominant position.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> In July 1999 the Premier League's method of selling rights collectively for all member clubs was investigated by the UK Restrictive Practices Court, who concluded that the agreement was not contrary to the public interest.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The BBC's highlights package on Saturday and Sunday nights, as well as other evenings when fixtures justify, will run until 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> Television rights alone for the period 2010 to 2013 have been purchased for £1.782&#160;billion.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> On 22 June 2009, due to troubles encountered by Setanta Sports after it failed to meet a final deadline over a £30&#160;million payment to the Premier League, <a href="/wiki/ESPN_(UK)" class="mw-redirect" title="ESPN (UK)">ESPN</a> was awarded two packages of UK rights containing 46 matches that were available for the 2009–10 season as well as a package of 23 matches per season from 2010 to 2011 to 2012–13.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> On 13 June 2012, the Premier League announced that <a href="/wiki/BT_Group" title="BT Group">BT</a> had been awarded 38 games a season for the 2013–14 through 2015–16 seasons at £246&#160;million-a-year. The remaining 116 games were retained by <a href="/wiki/Sky_plc" class="mw-redirect" title="Sky plc">Sky</a> who paid £760&#160;million-a-year. The total domestic rights have raised £3.018&#160;billion, an increase of 70.2% over the 2010–11 to 2012–13 rights.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> The value of the licensing deal rose by another 70.2% in 2015, when Sky and BT paid £5.136&#160;billion to renew their contracts with the Premier League for another three years up to the 2018–19 season.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><b>UK highlights</b>
</p>
<table class="wikitable">
